        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6fa3926530a0416facf25b3b4787db68">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '05051a5d-9c54-475b-b0b4-dbb44a18faa0'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d137875631" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The significance of exploring optimal electrode materials cannot be overstated, particularly in mitigating the critical issues posed by sluggish redox kinetics, significant volume variations, and severe structural collapse resulting from the insertion and extraction of sodium ions. These efforts are crucial for enhancing the longevity and rapid charging capabilities of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a defect engineering strategy for the in situ encapsulation of single-phase ternary iron phosphoselenide into porous carbon by robust chemical bonds with the formation of rod-like multicavity nanohybrids (FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C) is presented. The incorporation of Se atom not only modulates the electronic structure of the central metal Fe atom and enhances the intrinsic electrical conductivity, but also generates numerous additional reaction sites and accelerates the reaction kinetics of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, as corroborated by theoretical calculations and kinetic analysis. Notably, the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C demonstrates an outstanding rate capability of 321.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and long cycling stability over 1000 cycles. The sodium-ion full cell, pairing the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C anode with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode, exhibits a remarkable energy density of 202&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating its practical applicability. This work provides a controllable defect and morphology engineering strategy to construct advanced materials with fast charge transfer for high-power/energy SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404320-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404320-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The ever-increasing energy demand and concerns about limited lithium resources drive the development of various sustainable energy storage technologies. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered promising candidates for large-scale and fast-charging energy storage due to their relatively low cost, abundance of sodium resources, and the higher polarizability of Na<sup>+</sup> ion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0001_R_d137875623e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Matter eScience">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the sluggish redox kinetics and substantial electrode volume change during cycling, caused by the relatively large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (1.02&nbsp;Å) compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ion (0.67&nbsp;Å), lead to low Coulombic efficiency, inadequate cycle stability and inferior rate capability, thereby impeding practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0002_R_d137875623e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Natl. Sci. Rev. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, one of the key tasks in the development of fast-charging SIBs is to explore suitable Na<sup>+</sup> host anode materials with rapid kinetic response, high and stable reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0003_R_d137875623e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, conversion-type anode materials such as metal sulfides, selenides, and phosphides have garnered significant research enthusiasm owing to their fascinating merits of high theoretical specific capacity and medium voltage plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0004_R_d137875623e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Power Sources J. Alloys Compd. Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the intrinsic mediocre conductivity and drastic structural collapse during conversion reaction usually causes severe electrode pulverization and repeated solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) fracture/regrowth, resulting in slow Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics and fast capacity decay. To tackle these issues, various strategies have been investigated, including optimizing the size to nanoscale, modifying conductive carbon skeletons, and combining multiple phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0005_R_d137875623e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite substantial progress, a series of fatal shortcomings inevitably accompany these advancements, as follows: i) the nanosized nanoparticles, with elevated specific surface energy, often evoke severe particle aggregation and increased electrolyte consumption, leading to a low initial coulomb efficiency; ii) the weak interfacial coupling force between nonpolar carbon frame and polar active components induces active material detachment from the carbon matrix upon the sodiation/desodiation, significantly impacting cycle life; iii) multiple phases hybrid structure with unstable phase boundaries usually increases the interfacial side reactions.</p>
         
         <p>Single-phase ternary metal-phosphorus chalcogenides (MPX, X = S, Se), created through the introduction of exogenous anionic ligands, have been demonstrated great potential for enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> storage ability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0006_R_d137875623e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the electronic structure feature of MPX can be delicately regulate through the atomic level interface engineering, resulting in higher chemical diversity and faster electronic/ionic conductivity compared to the MP and MX materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0007_R_d137875623e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the strategy of anion doping/substitution can further tune the lattice defect, generating abundant electrochemical activity sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ion. Until now, a few ternary MPX anode materials, such as MnPS<sub>3</sub>/rGO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0008_R_d137875623e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Alloys Compd.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CoPS@C@N-CNF,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0009_R_d137875623e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>/C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0010_R_d137875623e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SnP<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>/graphene<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0011_R_d137875623e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and BiPS<sub>4</sub>-CNT,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0012_R_d137875623e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have emerged and exhibited promising electrochemical performance in SIBs systems. It should be noted that the larger atomic radius and stronger shielding effect of Se, along with the weaker metal-Se bond, could potentially endow the MPSe with enhanced electronic conductivity and conversion reaction kinetics than the MPS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0013_R_d137875623e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, achieving single-phase MPSe with controllable Se substitution content is still challenging and rarely reported for SIBs, especially for polyanion-doped iron-based compound. Moeover, the underlying relationship and detailed mechanism between the anion Se ligand and Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage properties have yet to be disclosed.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we successfully synthesized single-phase FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C rod-like multicavity nanohybrids using a one-step in situ phosphorylation/selenization strategy. The combined presence of Se and P anion ligand synergistically influences the electronic structure of the central metal Fe atom in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, thereby enhancing the electrochemical reaction kinetics and lowering the conversion reaction potential. The unique rod-like multicavity structure not only prevents the FePSe<sub>3</sub> active components aggregation but also accelerates ion/electron transport and mitigates volume changes during cycling. With these unique architectures and composition properties, the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C electrode exhibits superior fast-charging ability at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, high reversible capacities and long-term cycling stability as an anode material for for both half and full SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404320-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404320-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis strategy for FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composites is depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1a</a>. Initially, highly uniform iron-based MOF (MIL-88A) hexagonal prisms are synthesized. The MIL-88A precursor, obtained through a modified hydrothermal method, exhibits a smooth surface with an average length of ≈3–5&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a–c</a>, Supporting Information). Afterward, the MIL-88A is subjected to a tube furnace filled with a reducing atmosphere and reduced to metallic iron at high temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d–f</a>, Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that the initial development of a multicavity structure occurs after the high-temperature cleavage of the MIL-88A precursor, and this phenomenon can be observed in field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images. This is attributed to the collapse of the organic framework of the MIL-88A precursor at high temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0014_R_d137875623e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater. Chem. Phys. Chem. Eng. J. RSC Adv.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the formation of metallic iron and the development of graphitized carbon shells take place, leading to the emergence of a multicavity-like structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0015_R_d137875623e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon synchronous high-temperature phosphorization/selenization annealing, the Fe precursor undergoes transformation to become FePSe<sub>3</sub>, being in-situ encapsulated within the porous carbon framework, thereby forming a more pronounced multicavity structure. For comparison, a similar synthesis route has been employed for the preparation of FeP@C and FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C with some modifications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404320-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/755a36c7-ea12-4fa4-9a24-d65108a10f6e/adfm202404320-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/755a36c7-ea12-4fa4-9a24-d65108a10f6e/adfm202404320-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/527b4c6c-0522-4e39-8e0d-117d5f28110a/adfm202404320-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/755a36c7-ea12-4fa4-9a24-d65108a10f6e/adfm202404320-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404320-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404320" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the formation process, b,c) FESEM images, d,e) TEM images, f) selected-area electron diffraction patterns, g) HRTEM image of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composite, h) HRTEM image of FeP@C composite, and i–m) EDS elemental mappings of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The FESEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C display a rod-like morphology with a porous surface and well-defined multicavity structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1b–d</a>). The magnified TEM image further reveals that the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C exhibits a hollow internal cavity and is encapsulated in a porous carbon matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0016_R_d137875623e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern displays multiple diffraction rings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1f</a>), indicating the polycrystalline nature of multicavity FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C nanostructures. The diffraction rings observed in the SAED pattern can be indexed to the (006), (11-3), (21-2), and (300) crystal planes of FePSe<sub>3</sub>, consistent with XRD analysis. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image clearly shows the presence of a carbon layer, which wraps the surfaces of the FePSe<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1g</a>). Additionally, lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.28&nbsp;nm are identified, aligning well with the (11-3) plane of FePSe<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0017_R_d137875623e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the doping of large-sized Se atoms induces disorder and distortion in the crystal structure, leading to the generation of a large number of crystal defects that are observable in the HRTEM image of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C and use different colours for lattice stripe marking processes. For comparison, the FeP@C exhibits a smaller crystal pitch (0.24&nbsp;nm) and fewer defects than FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1h</a>). The high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) image of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mappings reveal the homogeneous dispersion of FePSe<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles within the multicavity rod-like structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0001">1i–m</a>). The EDX energy spectrum results show that the atomic ratio of Fe:P:Se in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C was calculated to be ≈1:1:3 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This ratio is consistent with the results of elemental analyses and corresponds to the stoichiometric ratio of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, confirming the successful introduction of Se species into the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C lattice. Additionally, various iron-based composites were obtained by adjusting the ratio of phosphorus and selenium sources. The morphology of these materials was revealed by SEM and TEM observations, and all these composites also exhibit a rod-like multicavity structure (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, pure Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C was obtained without the addition of a phosphorus source, and its XRD and SEM results are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), demonstrating the preservation of rod morphology.</p>
         
         <p>The crystallographic features and phase compositions of the resulting products are identified through XRD measurements. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2a</a>, all reflected signals from the sample FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C can be attributed to hexagonal FePSe<sub>3</sub> with an R-3 (148) space group (JCPDS# 89–1415).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0002_R_d137875623e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Natl. Sci. Rev. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noteworthy that the characteristic peak of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C located at 31.6 degrees is shifted to a lower angle compared to FeP@C after the introduction of large-size Se into FeP@C. This shift is primarily attributed to the widening of the lattice spacing, further indicating that Se and P are mixed at the atomic level.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0006_R_d137875623e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to further investigate the crystal structure of the products, Raman spectroscopy was conducted, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2b</a>. The Raman spectrum of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C exhibits three characteristic peaks. The vibrational modes mainly originate from the D<sub>3</sub>d symmetry (P<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>6</sub>) unit. The peak at 141.5&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the E<sub>g</sub> phonon mode, while the peaks at 161.5 and 208.8&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the two A<sub>1g</sub> phonon modes. Both E<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes involve vibrations of P-Se bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0018_R_d137875623e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the A<sub>1g</sub> modes indicate the formation of layered FePSe<sub>3</sub>, which contributes to the rapid de-embedding of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0019_R_d137875623e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pronounced peaks observed at 210.8 and 272.7&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C and FeP@C spectra can be attributed to the Fe-P vibration. Additionally, in the FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C spectra, the subtle peak at 174.4&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to Se-Se correlation, providing further evidence for the formation of both FeP and Fe3Se<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0007_R_d137875623e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The two prominent peaks at 1343.0 and 1572.3&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C material are assigned to sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized disordered carbon (D-band) and sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized graphitic carbon (G-band), respectively. Moreover, the calculated I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values for FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C, and FeP@C are ≈1.09, 1.05, and 1.03, respectively. The elevated strength ratio suggests a higher disordered carbon content in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composites, leading to an increased concentration of defects within the carbon layer of the composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0020_R_d137875623e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2c</a>, the depicted layered crystal structure showcases the hexagonal arrangement of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, resembling that of FeP@C, where Se (yellow) substitutes certain P (blue) atoms. The incorporation of Se doping introduces extra distortions in the crystal grid, resulting in an expanded layer spacing that facilitates rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) tests confirmed the existence of Se vacancies (<i>V</i><sub>Se</sub>) in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2d</a>). Specifically, the Lorentz curve at a g value of 2.003 is linked to <i>V</i><sub>Se</sub>, originating from heterogeneous interactions between FeP@C and FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C. The stronger EPR signal in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C further indicates a higher concentration of defects and a higher number of active sites when compared to FeP@C and FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0021_R_d137875623e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The adsorption-desorption curves exhibit a characteristic type-IV profile featuring a hysteresis loop within the relative pressure range of 0.46-0.94, indicating the presence of numerous meso- and micropores in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0022_R_d137875623e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C possess a higher specific Brunaurer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area of 53.59&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> than those of the FeP@C (10.96&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C (8.81&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) pore size distribution map illustrates that the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C possesses a more diverse range of pore types, including microporous, mesoporous, and macroporous structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2f</a>). The high porosity not only offers abundant channels for the diffusion and storage of Na<sup>+</sup> throughout the entire electrode but also provides sufficient buffer space to mitigate the volume expansion of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C during cycling. The carbon matrix content in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C was determined through thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) under air conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The residual product remaining after oxidation during TGA measurements was confirmed to be pure FPO<sub>4</sub>, as indicated by the corresponding XRD pattern. This resulted in FePSe<sub>3</sub> and carbon mass fractions in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composite of ≈76.1% and 23.9%, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404320-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c10bea23-9f89-4f25-9093-1c972e075126/adfm202404320-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c10bea23-9f89-4f25-9093-1c972e075126/adfm202404320-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/39a27f6a-3a3f-4751-9727-99f6b5ff1d10/adfm202404320-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c10bea23-9f89-4f25-9093-1c972e075126/adfm202404320-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404320-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404320" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C<sub>,</sub> FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C and FeP@C. c) Schematic diagram of crystal structure of FePSe<sub>3</sub>. d) electron paramagnetic resonance results, e) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isothermal curves and f) pore size distribution of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C<sub>,</sub> FeP/Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C and FeP@C. g) High-resolution XPS spectra of Fe 2p, and h) high-resolution XPS spectra of P 2p for FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C and FeP@C. i) High-resolution XPS spectrum of Se 3d in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were employed to assess the surface electron states and functional groups of the prepared samples. The detailed XPS spectra are instrumental in identifying the primary characteristic signals corresponding to the elements Fe, P, Se, C, and O (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution C 1s spectrum, distinct signals at binding energies of 284.5, 287.4, and 288.8&nbsp;eV reveal the presence of C-C, C-P, and C = O, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c,d</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0023_R_d137875623e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2g</a>, the Fe 2p peaks of FeP@C exhibit two distinct pairs of peaks: one at 710.8 and 723.3&nbsp;eV, attributed to the presence of Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and the other at 712.9 and 726.4&nbsp;eV, associated with oxidized species likely formed by the superficial oxidation of iron phosphide upon exposure to air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0024_R_d137875623e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano Mater. Chem. Front. Carbon">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the peak at 708.5 and 720.5&nbsp;eV indicates the existence of the metallic Fe, which may be generated by the minor reduction of selenium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0004_R_d137875623e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Power Sources J. Alloys Compd. Nano Energy">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0025_R_d137875623e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Energy Lett.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noteworthy that after Se doping, the existence of Fe<sup>3+</sup> proves advantageous for the formation of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C. The Fe 2p peak of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C is shifted to a lower binding energy, attributable to the longer bond length of Se-Se and the lower electronegativity of Se atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0026_R_d137875623e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the P 2p high-resolution spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2h</a>), a similar phenomenon is observed, wherein the P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C are shifted to lower energies compared to those in FeP@C. Correspondingly, two peaks at 129.8 and 130.9&nbsp;eV are associated with P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of Fe-P bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0027_R_d137875623e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak observed at 132.9&nbsp;eV may be attributed to the formation of P-C bonds, possibly arising from the interaction between phosphorus and the carbon substrate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0028_R_d137875623e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the peak at 134.04&nbsp;eV may be related to oxidized P species resulting from exposure to air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0029_R_d137875623e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Alloys Compd.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, a distinctive peak appears at 137.8&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the characteristic P-Se bond, certifying the presence of a strong interaction between Fe-P and the incorporated Se atoms in the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0007_R_d137875623e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0013_R_d137875623e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0017_R_d137875623e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0018_R_d137875623e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution Se 3d spectra of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0002">2i</a> present two spin-orbit doublets at the binding energies of 54.5 and 56.9&nbsp;eV associating with the Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, which are identified as the characteristic signal of Fe-Se and Se-Se bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0030_R_d137875623e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The above results unveil strong chemical bonding and electron transfer within the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C architecture, indicating favorable electronic conductivity in the multicavity nanostructure.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties for sodium ion storage of the as-fabricated composite anodes are initially evaluated using half-coin cells with metallic sodium foil serving as the counter/reference electrode. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves for the first three cycles of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C and FeP@C at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3&nbsp;V. The prominent reduction peak observed at ≈1.4&nbsp;V, which diminishes in subsequent cycles during the initial cathodic scan, is associated with the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase film and the conversion reaction represented by Na<sub>x</sub>FePSe<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0002_R_d137875623e1075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Natl. Sci. Rev. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The faint peak at 1.1&nbsp;V could result from the irreversible cleavage of the Se-P bond, leading to the generation of Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> and P.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0031_R_d137875623e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the conversion of Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Fe may contribute to the prominent reduction peak ≈0.9&nbsp;V. The peaks located ≈0.2&nbsp;V are mainly caused by further sodiation of P to form Na<sub>3</sub>P.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0004_R_d137875623e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Power Sources J. Alloys Compd. Nano Energy">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0032_R_d137875623e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nanoscale">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the anodic scan, three distinct peaks were identified at 1.5, 1.7, and 2.1&nbsp;V, indicating a multistep denaturation process involving Na<sub>3</sub>P, Fe, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. This process leads to the formation of P and Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub>, contributing to the capacity in subsequent cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0031_R_d137875623e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0033_R_d137875623e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reference sample of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, that is, the FeP@C has a similar redox mechanism, which is also evaluated in this work (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>). In the subsequent cycles, FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C exhibited nearly overlapping redox peaks, accompanied by a larger CV curve area when compared to FeP@C. This observation suggests enhanced stability and reversibility in the sodium ion storage reaction process following the introduction of Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0034_R_d137875623e1125" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further illustrate the sodium storage mechanism of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, the CV curves of the Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@C electrode depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a> (Supporting Information), reveals a nuanced progression. Initially, a subtle peak emerges ≈2.09&nbsp;V during the cathodic scanning process, primarily attributed to a side reaction at the electrode interface, yielding a solid electrolyte interfacial film. As Na<sup>+</sup> continuously intercalate into the Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> lattice, the formation of Na<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> ensues, manifesting in a robust peak at ≈1.17&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0035_R_d137875623e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further into the discharge process, a broad peak materializes ≈0.82&nbsp;V, indicative of the transformation reaction from Na<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> to metallic iron and Na<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0036_R_d137875623e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J. Carbon">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During subsequent anodic scanning, the emergence of strong peaks ≈1.52&nbsp;V is attributed to the conversion of metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>Se into Na<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0037_R_d137875623e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Appl. Surf. Sci. Electrochim. Acta">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, peaks observed at 2.04 and 2.32&nbsp;V denote the deintercalation reaction and the formation of Fe<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0035_R_d137875623e1191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0038_R_d137875623e1191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Small">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C displays a smaller voltage difference during the conversion reaction (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3a</a>), demonstrating accelerated redox reaction kinetics resulting from Se doping.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404320-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3899abf3-16fc-45a8-8b4e-abb40f4e5978/adfm202404320-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3899abf3-16fc-45a8-8b4e-abb40f4e5978/adfm202404320-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/07ece17e-f235-4602-8ffa-d9716340022f/adfm202404320-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3899abf3-16fc-45a8-8b4e-abb40f4e5978/adfm202404320-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404320-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404320" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C and FeP@C electrodes at a scan rate 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for the 2nd cycle. b) In situ XRD patterns of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C electrode during the initial cycle. High-resolution XPS c) Fe 2p spectra, d) P 2p spectra and e) Se 3d spectra of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C electrode at different initial discharged and charged states. Ex situ HRTEM images of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C electrode at different initial discharged states: f) discharged state of 1.0&nbsp;V, g) discharged state of 0.01&nbsp;V, h) EDS elemental mappings of discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, and at different initial charged states: i) charged state of 1.5&nbsp;V, j) charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V, k)The corresponding schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism for the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To acquire a more profound and comprehensive insight into the initial voltage-dependent denaturation mechanism of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C, we conducted in situ/ex situ measurements during the sodiation/desodiation processes. The in-situ XRD patterns are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, multiple reflections of BeO and Be are discernible within the 2θ range of ≈35–50°. As the discharge progresses from the open circuit voltage to ≈1.0&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of the FePSe<sub>3</sub> phase gradually fade, accompanied by two weak peaks at ≈24.8° and 26.9°. These peaks correspond to crystal faces (020) and (210), indicating the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> and the formation of intermediate Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS # 74–0779). As the discharge continues, the appearance of the (111) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>Se at ≈22.6° (JCPDS # 77–2155) and the (111) plane of metallic Fe at ≈45.8° (JCPDS # 88–2324) indicates the occurrence of the conversion reaction from Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Fe. Furthermore, the detection of the Na<sub>3</sub>P (110) peak signal at ≈36.1° (JCPDS #74-1164) suggests the existence of an alloying reaction between P and Na<sup>+</sup>. Upon reaching a discharge of 0.01&nbsp;V, signifying the fully sodiated state, the peaks associated with Na<sub>2</sub>Se, Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P attain their maximum intensity, and no additional peaks are observed. Subsequently, the potential was incrementally raised to ≈1.5&nbsp;V. The anticipated reverse conversion and reverse alloying reactions became apparent, substantiated by the gradual fading of the Na<sub>2</sub>Se, Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P peaks and the resurgence of the Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> peaks. When further charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, the Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> peak gradually intensified, and no other peaks manifested, signifying that the ultimate product of the desodiation process was Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub>. This finding agrees with the reported use of FePS<sub>3</sub> as an anode material for SIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0031_R_d137875623e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0033_R_d137875623e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, no distinctive reflective signals arising from phosphorus were identified, possibly attributed to its minute microcrystalline size or amorphous nature.</p>
         
         <p>Subsequently, ex-situ XPS was conducted to further analyze the valence changes of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C during cycling. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3c</a>, the high-resolution Fe 2p spectra reveal that as the electrode undergoes discharge from 1.0 to the 0.01&nbsp;V, the Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p peaks exhibit a noticeable shift toward lower binding energies. Of particular significance, a discernible characteristic peak arising from Fe<sup>0</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> was observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0039_R_d137875623e1316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following this, upon charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, Fe<sup>0</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> completely vanishes, and a subtle shift of the Fe 2p peak toward higher binding energy is observed. The high-resolution spectrum of P 2p is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3d</a>, revealing that the Fe-P bond at 131.2&nbsp;eV entirely disappears from 1.0&nbsp;V upon discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V and remains absent upon reaching full charge, which observation strongly supports the notion of irreversible fracture of the Fe-P bond, as described earlier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0023_R_d137875623e1329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the high-resolution spectrum of Se 3d during the initial sodiation-desodiation process. Observing a significant shift toward lower binding energies from 1.0&nbsp;V to the fully discharged state, it suggests the presence of intense chemical bonding between Na<sup>+</sup> and the heteroatom doping active sites in the peak of the Se 3d spectrum.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0040_R_d137875623e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energ Environ. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The physical phase transformation of FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C was further verified by ex-situ HRTEM characterization. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3f</a>, the FePSe<sub>3</sub> phase undergoes complete disappearance after discharging to 1.0&nbsp;V. However, discernible lattice fringes of 0.325 and 0.202&nbsp;nm become apparent in the HRTEM image, corresponding to the (−202) and (−133) faces of Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub>. When further discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3g</a>), the ultimate products exhibit crystal face spacings of 0.308, 0.241, and 0.198&nbsp;nm. These are associated with the (102) face of Na<sub>3</sub>P, the (220) face of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and the (111) face of metallic Fe, respectively, indicating the taking place of transformation and alloying reactions. Moreover, the HAADF-STEM and EDX mapping images displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3h</a> reveal that the rod-like multicavity structure can be well maintained, and the elements Fe, P, Se, and Na are uniformly distributed in the C matrix, demonstrating its high structural stability. Following the charging process to 1.5&nbsp;V, Na<sub>2</sub>Se underwent partial transformation into Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub>, with some Na<sub>2</sub>Se remaining. Simultaneously, the lattice fringe of Na<sub>3</sub>P disappeared, forming P monomers, indicative of a reversible reaction involving phosphorus.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0023_R_d137875623e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0033_R_d137875623e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3i</a>, the observed lattice stripes correspond to the (210) face of Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub>, the (311) face of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and the (111) face of P. This alignment provides additional evidence supporting the reaction. Upon charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, the residual Na<sub>2</sub>Se was completely converted into Na<sub>3</sub>FeSe<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3j</a>). At the same time, selected diffraction patterns at different potentials have been marked (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), and the crystallographic findings align consistently with the ex situ TEMs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0004_R_d137875623e1407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Power Sources J. Alloys Compd. Nano Energy">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0024_R_d137875623e1407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano Mater. Chem. Front. Carbon">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202404320-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404320-bib-0035_R_d137875623e1407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates a reversible phase transition of the FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C composite anode during Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. Combined with the above discussion, the sodium storage mechanism in FePSe<sub>3</sub>@C can be visually represented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404320-fig-0003">3k</a>. The associated reactions are summarized as follows:

                           <mi>Se</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{x}}}{\mathrm{FePS}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{\mathrm{3}}}  + \left( {{\mathrm{9 - x}}} \right) {\mathrm{N}} {{{\mathrm{a}}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}  + \left( {{\mathrm{9 - x}}} \right)\,{{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - } \to {\mathrm{Fe}} + \,{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{3}}}{\mathrm{P}}  + {\mathrm{3N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{Se}} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404320-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f1ba0094-1948-4343-8df9-e6fce3427bfd/adfm202404320-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404320:adfm202404320-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404320-math-0003.png">

                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{3}}}{\mathrm{P}} \leftrightarrow {\mathrm{P}} + {\mathrm{3N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^{{ + }}} + {\mathrm{3}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - }\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404320-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7ca548e9-48bf-482a-90df-f26f856b00a6/adfm202404320-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404320:adfm202404320-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404320-math-0004.png">

                        <mrow>
                           <mi>Fe</mi>

                           </mrow>
